<div class="thumb tright">

<p><b>Hamburg</b> (<small>German pronunciation:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈhambʊɐ̯k]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:GeoTrinity_Hamburg.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/GeoTrinity_Hamburg.ogg" class="internal" title="GeoTrinity Hamburg.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>, <small>local pronunciation:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ˈhambʊɪ̯ç]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:GeoTrinity_Hamburch.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d8/GeoTrinity_Hamburch.ogg" class="internal" title="GeoTrinity Hamburch.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>; <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a>/<a href="/wiki/West_Low_German" title="West Low German">Low Saxon</a>: <i>Hamborg</i> <small>—&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ˈhambɔːç]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:GT_Hamborch.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/GT_Hamborch.ogg" class="internal" title="GT Hamborch.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>),<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[a]</a></sup> officially the <b>Free and Hanseatic City of Hamburg</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Freie und Hansestadt Hamburg</span>),<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-6">[5]</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Germany_by_population" title="List of cities in Germany by population">second largest city</a> and a <a href="/wiki/States_of_Germany" title="States of Germany">state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> with a population of over 1.7 million people.</p>
<p>The official name reflects <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hamburg" title="History of Hamburg">Hamburg's history</a> as a member of the medieval <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">free imperial city</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, a <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a>, and one of the 16 states of Germany. Before the 1871 <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">Unification of Germany</a>, it was a fully <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a> state. Prior to the constitutional changes in 1919, the civic republic was ruled by a class of hereditary <a href="/wiki/Grand_burgher" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand burgher">grand burghers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hanseaten_(class)" title="Hanseaten (class)">Hanseaten</a>. Though repeatedly destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Hamburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Fire of Hamburg">Great Fire of Hamburg</a>, the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_flood_of_1962" title="North Sea flood of 1962">floods</a>, and military conflicts including <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Hamburg_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Hamburg in World War II">WW2 bombing raids</a>, the city managed to recover and emerge wealthier after each catastrophe.</p>
<p>On the river <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">Elbe</a>, Hamburg is a major port<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> and a global service, media, logistics and industrial hub, with headquarters and facilities of <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blohm_%2B_Voss" class="mw-redirect" title="Blohm + Voss">Blohm + Voss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aurubis" title="Aurubis">Aurubis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beiersdorf" title="Beiersdorf">Beiersdorf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unilever" title="Unilever">Unilever</a>. The radio and television broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Norddeutscher_Rundfunk" title="Norddeutscher Rundfunk">NDR</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gruner_%2B_Jahr" title="Gruner + Jahr">Gruner + Jahr</a> (Europe's largest printing and publishing firm), <a href="/wiki/Der_Spiegel" title="Der Spiegel">Der Spiegel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Die_Zeit" title="Die Zeit">Die Zeit</a> are also based in Hamburg. Hamburg has been an important financial centre for centuries and is the seat of Germany's oldest <a href="/wiki/Hamburg_Stock_Exchange" title="Hamburg Stock Exchange">stock exchange</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_banks_in_continuous_operation" title="List of oldest banks in continuous operation">world's second oldest bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berenberg_Bank" title="Berenberg Bank">Berenberg Bank</a>. With the <a href="/wiki/International_Tribunal_for_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="International Tribunal for the Law of the Sea">International Tribunal for the Law of the Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/EU-LAC_Foundation" title="EU-LAC Foundation">EU-LAC Foundation</a>, the UNESCO Institute of Lifelong Learning, many <a href="/wiki/List_of_diplomatic_missions_in_Hamburg" title="List of diplomatic missions in Hamburg">consular and diplomatic missions</a>, and various international conferences like <a href="/wiki/Hamburg_Summit:_China_meets_Europe" title="Hamburg Summit: China meets Europe">Hamburg Summit: China meets Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/2017_G20_Hamburg_summit" title="2017 G20 Hamburg summit">2017 G20 Hamburg summit</a>, the city is also a factor in world politics and international law.</p>
<p>The city is a <a href="#Tourism">tourist destination</a> for both domestic and international visitors, <a href="/wiki/Mercer_Quality_of_Living_Survey" title="Mercer Quality of Living Survey">ranked 18th</a> in the world for <a href="/wiki/Livability" class="mw-redirect" title="Livability">livability</a> in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Speicherstadt" title="Speicherstadt">Speicherstadt</a> and <i>Kontorhausviertel</i> were declared a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamburg is a major European <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Hamburg" title="Education in Hamburg">science, research, and education hub</a>, with several universities and institutes. Its creative industries and cultural sites include the <a href="/wiki/Elbphilharmonie" title="Elbphilharmonie">Elbphilharmonie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laeiszhalle" title="Laeiszhalle">Laeisz</a> concert halls, <a href="/wiki/List_of_museums_in_Hamburg#Art" title="List of museums in Hamburg">art venues</a>, music producers, and artists. It gave birth to movements like <i><a href="/wiki/Hamburger_Schule" title="Hamburger Schule">Hamburger Schule</a></i> and paved the way for bands including <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_in_Hamburg" title="The Beatles in Hamburg">The Beatles</a>. Hamburg is also known for several <a href="/wiki/List_of_theatres_in_Hamburg" title="List of theatres in Hamburg">theatres</a> and a variety of musical shows. <a href="/wiki/St._Pauli" title="St. Pauli">St. Pauli</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Reeperbahn" title="Reeperbahn">Reeperbahn</a> is among the best known European entertainment districts.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hamburg is on the southern point of the <a href="/wiki/Jutland_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Jutland Peninsula">Jutland Peninsula</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a> to the south and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> to the north, with the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> to the west and the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> to the north-east. It is on the River <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">Elbe</a> at its confluence with the <a href="/wiki/Alster" title="Alster">Alster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bille_(Elbe)" title="Bille (Elbe)">Bille</a>. The city centre is around the <a href="/wiki/Binnenalster" title="Binnenalster">Binnenalster</a> ("Inner Alster") and <a href="/wiki/Au%C3%9Fenalster" title="Außenalster">Außenalster</a> ("Outer Alster"), both formed by damming the River Alster to create lakes. The islands of <a href="/wiki/Neuwerk" title="Neuwerk">Neuwerk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scharh%C3%B6rn" title="Scharhörn">Scharhörn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeh%C3%B6rn" title="Nigehörn">Nigehörn</a>, 100 kilometres (60&#160;mi) away in the <a href="/wiki/Wadden_Sea_National_Parks" title="Wadden Sea National Parks">Hamburg Wadden Sea National Park</a>, are also part of the city of Hamburg.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The neighbourhoods of <a href="/wiki/Neuenfelde" title="Neuenfelde">Neuenfelde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cranz,_Hamburg" title="Cranz, Hamburg">Cranz</a>, Francop and <a href="/wiki/Finkenwerder" title="Finkenwerder">Finkenwerder</a> are part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Altes_Land" title="Altes Land">Altes Land</a></i> (old land) region, the largest contiguous fruit-producing region in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>. <a href="/wiki/Neugraben-Fischbek" title="Neugraben-Fischbek">Neugraben-Fischbek</a> has Hamburg's highest elevation, the <a href="/wiki/Hasselbrack" title="Hasselbrack">Hasselbrack</a> at 116.2 metres (381&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">AMSL</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Hamburg borders the <a href="/wiki/States_of_Germany" title="States of Germany">states</a> of <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">Lower Saxony</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hamburg has an <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a> (<i>Cfb</i>), influenced by its proximity to the coast and marine air masses that originate over the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>. Nearby wetlands also enjoy a maritime temperate climate. Snowfall differs a lot in the past decades: while in the late 1970s and early 1980s, heavy snowfall occurred,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> the winters of recent years have been less cold, with snowfall on several days per year.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The warmest months are June, July, and August, with high temperatures of 20.1 to 22.5&#160;°C (68.2 to 72.5&#160;°F). The coldest are December, January, and February, with low temperatures of −0.3 to 1.0&#160;°C (31.5 to 33.8&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-WMO_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-16">[15]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hamburg" title="History of Hamburg">History of Hamburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Hamburg" title="Timeline of Hamburg">Timeline of Hamburg</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Limes.saxoniae.wmt.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Limes.saxoniae.wmt.png/220px-Limes.saxoniae.wmt.png" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Limes.saxoniae.wmt.png/330px-Limes.saxoniae.wmt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Limes.saxoniae.wmt.png/440px-Limes.saxoniae.wmt.png 2x" data-file-width="795" data-file-height="515" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Limes.saxoniae.wmt.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Limes_Saxoniae" title="Limes Saxoniae">Limes Saxoniae</a></i> border between the <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a> and the Slavic <a href="/wiki/Obotrites" title="Obotrites">Obotrites</a>, established about 810.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamburg_in_1150.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Hamburg_in_1150.jpg/220px-Hamburg_in_1150.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Hamburg_in_1150.jpg/330px-Hamburg_in_1150.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Hamburg_in_1150.jpg/440px-Hamburg_in_1150.jpg 2x" data-file-width="981" data-file-height="616" /></a>

Hamburg in 1150</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" class="mw-redirect" title="Claudius Ptolemy">Claudius Ptolemy</a> (2nd century AD) reported the first name for the vicinity as <b>Treva</b><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name Hamburg comes from the first permanent building on the site, a <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castle</a> which the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> ordered constructed in AD 808. It rose on rocky terrain in a marsh between the <a href="/wiki/Alster" title="Alster">River Alster</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">River Elbe</a> as a defence against <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic</a> incursion, and acquired the name <i>Hammaburg</i>, <i>burg</i> meaning castle or fort. The origin of the <i>Hamma</i> term remains uncertain,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> as does the exact location of the castle.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Hamburg">Medieval Hamburg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medieval Hamburg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 834, Hamburg was designated as the seat of a <a href="/wiki/Prince-Bishop" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince-Bishop">bishopric</a>. The first bishop, <a href="/wiki/Ansgar" title="Ansgar">Ansgar</a>, became known as the Apostle of the North. Two years later, Hamburg was united with <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Bremen">Bremen</a> as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_administrators,_archbishops,_bishops,_and_prince-archbishops_of_Bremen" title="List of administrators, archbishops, bishops, and prince-archbishops of Bremen">Bishopric of Hamburg-Bremen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-verg15_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verg15-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamburg was destroyed and occupied several times. In 845, 600 <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> ships sailed up the <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">River Elbe</a> and destroyed Hamburg, at that time a town of around 500 inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-verg15_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verg15-21">[20]</a></sup> In 1030, King <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_II_Lambert" title="Mieszko II Lambert">Mieszko II Lambert</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">Poland</a> burned down the city. <a href="/wiki/Valdemar_II_of_Denmark" title="Valdemar II of Denmark">Valdemar II of Denmark</a> raided and occupied Hamburg in 1201 and in 1214. The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> killed at least 60% of the population in 1350.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Hamburg experienced several great fires in the medieval period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seal_City_of_Hamburg_1241_replica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Seal_City_of_Hamburg_1241_replica.jpg/220px-Seal_City_of_Hamburg_1241_replica.jpg" width="220" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Seal_City_of_Hamburg_1241_replica.jpg/330px-Seal_City_of_Hamburg_1241_replica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Seal_City_of_Hamburg_1241_replica.jpg/440px-Seal_City_of_Hamburg_1241_replica.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2868" data-file-height="2736" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jmdavid_hamburg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Jmdavid_hamburg.jpg/220px-Jmdavid_hamburg.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Jmdavid_hamburg.jpg/330px-Jmdavid_hamburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Jmdavid_hamburg.jpg/440px-Jmdavid_hamburg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3747" data-file-height="2559" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jmdavid_hamburg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hamburg in 1811</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1189, by imperial charter, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I "Barbarossa"</a> granted Hamburg the status of an <a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">Imperial Free City</a> and tax-free access up<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the Lower Elbe into the North Sea. In 1265, an allegedly forged letter was presented to or by the Rath of Hamburg.<sup id="cite_ref-verg26_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verg26-23">[22]</a></sup> This charter, along with Hamburg's proximity to the main trade routes of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, quickly made it a major port in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>. Its trade alliance with <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a> in 1241 marks the origin and core of the powerful Hanseatic League of trading cities. On 8 November 1266, a contract between <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a> and Hamburg's traders allowed them to establish a <i>hanse</i> in London. This was the first time in history that the word <i>hanse</i> was used for the trading guild of the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-verg30_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verg30-24">[23]</a></sup> In 1270, the solicitor of the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Hamburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Senate of Hamburg">senate of Hamburg</a>, <i>Jordan von Boitzenburg</i>, wrote the first description of civil, criminal and procedural law for a city in Germany in the German language, the <i>Ordeelbook</i> (<i>Ordeel</i>: sentence).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> On 10 August 1410, civil unrest forced a compromise (German: <i>Rezeß</i>, literally meaning: withdrawal). This is considered the first <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Hamburg" title="Constitution of Hamburg">constitution of Hamburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> In 1529, the city embraced <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a>, and it received <a href="/wiki/Continental_Reformed_church" title="Continental Reformed church">Reformed</a> refugees from the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Valckenborgh" title="Jan van Valckenborgh">Jan van Valckenborgh</a> introduced a second layer to the fortifications to protect against the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty Years War">Thirty Years War</a> in the seventeenth century, he extended Hamburg and created a "New Town" (<i>Neustadt</i>) whose street names still date from the grid system of roads he introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Upon the dissolution of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> in 1806, the <a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">Free Imperial City</a> of Hamburg was not incorporated into a larger administrative area while retaining special privileges (<a href="/wiki/Mediatization" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediatization">mediatised</a>), but became a <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a> state with the official title of the <i>Free and Hanseatic City of Hamburg</i>. Hamburg was briefly annexed by <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I">Napoleon I</a> to the <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">First French Empire</a> (1804–1814/1815). Russian forces under <a href="/wiki/Levin_August,_Count_von_Bennigsen" class="mw-redirect" title="Levin August, Count von Bennigsen">General Bennigsen</a> finally freed the city in 1814. Hamburg re-assumed its pre-1811 status as a city-state in 1814. The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Vienna Congress</a> of 1815 confirmed Hamburg's independence and it became one of 39 sovereign states of the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a> (1815–1866).</p>
<p>In 1842, about a quarter of the inner city was destroyed in the "<a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Hamburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Fire of Hamburg">Great Fire</a>". The fire started on the night of 4 May and was not extinguished until 8 May. It destroyed three churches, the town hall, and many other buildings, killing 51 people and leaving an estimated 20,000 homeless. Reconstruction took more than 40 years.</p>
<p>After periodic political unrest, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">1848</a>, Hamburg adopted in 1860 a democratic constitution that provided for the election of the Senate, the governing body of the city-state, by adult taxpaying males. Other innovations included the separation of powers, the separation of Church and State, freedom of the press, of assembly and association. Hamburg became a member of the <a href="/wiki/North_German_Confederation" title="North German Confederation">North German Confederation</a> (1866–1871) and of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> (1871–1918), and maintained its self-ruling status during the Weimar Republic (1919–1933). The city experienced its fastest growth during the second half of the 19th century, when its population more than quadrupled to 800,000 as the growth of the city's <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> trade helped make it Europe's second-largest port. The <a href="/wiki/Hamburg_America_Line" title="Hamburg America Line">Hamburg-America Line</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Albert_Ballin" title="Albert Ballin">Albert Ballin</a> as its director, became the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">transatlantic</a> shipping company around the start of the 20th century. Shipping companies sailing to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> were based in the city. Hamburg was the departure port for many Germans and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europeans</a> to emigrate to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. Trading communities from all over the world established themselves there.</p>
<p>A major outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> in 1892 was badly handled by the city government, which retained an unusual degree of independence for a German city. About 8,600 died in the largest German epidemic of the late 19th century, and the last major cholera epidemic in a major city of the Western world.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Air_Force_Bomber_Command,_1942-1945._CL3400.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Royal_Air_Force_Bomber_Command%2C_1942-1945._CL3400.jpg/220px-Royal_Air_Force_Bomber_Command%2C_1942-1945._CL3400.jpg" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Royal_Air_Force_Bomber_Command%2C_1942-1945._CL3400.jpg/330px-Royal_Air_Force_Bomber_Command%2C_1942-1945._CL3400.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Royal_Air_Force_Bomber_Command%2C_1942-1945._CL3400.jpg/440px-Royal_Air_Force_Bomber_Command%2C_1942-1945._CL3400.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2143" data-file-height="1772" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Air_Force_Bomber_Command,_1942-1945._CL3400.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Hamburg_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Hamburg in World War II">Hamburg after the 1943 bombing</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamburg_Medienbunker_01_KMJ.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamburg_Medienbunker_01_KMJ.jpg/220px-Hamburg_Medienbunker_01_KMJ.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamburg_Medienbunker_01_KMJ.jpg/330px-Hamburg_Medienbunker_01_KMJ.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamburg_Medienbunker_01_KMJ.jpg/440px-Hamburg_Medienbunker_01_KMJ.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="478" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamburg_Medienbunker_01_KMJ.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Flak_tower" title="Flak tower">Flakturm</a></i> on the Heiligengeistfeld in Hamburg – one of four enormous fortress-like bunkers which were built of reinforced concrete between 1942 and 1944 and equipped with anti-aircraft artillery for air defense</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Third_Reich" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Reich">Third Reich</a> (1933–1945), Hamburg was a <i><a href="/wiki/Administrative_division_of_Nazi_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Administrative division of Nazi Germany">Gau</a></i> from 1934 until 1945. During the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, Hamburg suffered a series of Allied <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Hamburg_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Hamburg in World War II">air raids</a> which devastated much of the city and the harbour. On 23 July 1943, RAF firebombing created a <a href="/wiki/Firestorm" title="Firestorm">firestorm</a> which spread from the <i>Hauptbahnhof</i> (main railway station) and quickly moved south-east, completely destroying entire boroughs such as <a href="/wiki/Hammerbrook" title="Hammerbrook">Hammerbrook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billbrook" title="Billbrook">Billbrook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamm,_Hamburg" title="Hamm, Hamburg">Hamm South</a>. Thousands of people perished in these densely populated working class boroughs. While some of the boroughs destroyed were rebuilt as residential districts after the war, others such as Hammerbrook are nowadays purely commercial districts with almost no residential population. The raids, codenamed <a href="/wiki/Operation_Gomorrah" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Gomorrah">Operation Gomorrah</a> by the <a href="/wiki/RAF" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF">RAF</a>, killed at least 42,600 civilians; the precise number is not known. About one million civilians were evacuated in the aftermath of the raids.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hamburg_Commonwealth_War_Graves_Commission_Cemetery" title="Hamburg Commonwealth War Graves Commission Cemetery">Hamburg Commonwealth War Graves Commission Cemetery</a> is in the greater <a href="/wiki/Ohlsdorf_Cemetery" title="Ohlsdorf Cemetery">Ohlsdorf Cemetery</a> in the north of Hamburg.</p>
<p>At least 42,900 people are thought to have perished<sup id="cite_ref-kz-neuengamme_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kz-neuengamme-28">[27]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Neuengamme_concentration_camp" title="Neuengamme concentration camp">Neuengamme concentration camp</a> (about 25&#160;km (16&#160;mi) outside the city in the marshlands), mostly from epidemics and in the <a href="/wiki/SS_Cap_Arcona_(1927)" class="mw-redirect" title="SS Cap Arcona (1927)">bombing of Kriegsmarine evacuation vessels</a> by the Royal Air Force at the end of the war.</p>
<p>Hamburg had the greatest concentration of Jews in Germany. Systematic <a href="/wiki/Deportation#Deportation_in_the_Holocaust" title="Deportation">deportations of Jewish Germans and Gentile Germans of Jewish descent</a> started on 18 October 1941. These were all directed to <a href="/wiki/Ghettos_in_Nazi-occupied_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghettos in Nazi-occupied Europe">Ghettos in Nazi-occupied Europe</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">concentration camps</a>. Most deported persons perished in <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>. By the end of 1942 the <i>Jüdischer Religionsverband in Hamburg</i> was dissolved as an independent legal entity and its remaining assets and staff were assumed by the <a href="/wiki/Reichsvereinigung_der_Juden_in_Deutschland" title="Reichsvereinigung der Juden in Deutschland">Reichsvereinigung der Juden in Deutschland</a> (District Northwest). On 10 June 1943 the <a href="/wiki/Reichssicherheitshauptamt" class="mw-redirect" title="Reichssicherheitshauptamt">Reichssicherheitshauptamt</a> dissolved the <i>Reichsvereinigung</i> by a decree.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> The few remaining employees not somewhat protected by a <a href="/wiki/Anti-miscegenation_laws#Nazi_Germany" title="Anti-miscegenation laws">mixed marriage</a> were deported from Hamburg on 23 June to <a href="/wiki/Theresienstadt" class="mw-redirect" title="Theresienstadt">Theresienstadt</a>, where most of them perished.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_history">Post-war history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Post-war history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hamburg surrendered without a fight to <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">British Forces</a> on 3 May 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, Hamburg formed part of the <a href="/wiki/Allied-occupied_Germany" title="Allied-occupied Germany">British Zone of Occupation</a>; it became a state of the then <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">Federal Republic of Germany</a> in 1949.</p>
<p>From 1960 to 1962, <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a> launched their career by playing in various music clubs in the city.</p>
<p>On 16 February 1962, a <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_flood_of_1962" title="North Sea flood of 1962">North Sea flood</a> caused the Elbe to rise to an all-time high, inundating one-fifth of Hamburg and killing more than 300 people.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Inner_German_border" title="Inner German border">Inner German border</a> – only 50 kilometres (30&#160;mi) east of Hamburg – separated the city from most of its hinterland and reduced Hamburg's global trade. Since <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a> in 1990, and the accession of several <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central European</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a> into the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> in 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Hamburg" title="Port of Hamburg">Port of Hamburg</a> has restarted ambitions for regaining its position as the region's largest deep-sea port for container shipping and its major commercial and trading centre.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td>Total</td>
<td>631,246</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On 31 December 2016, there were 1,860,759 people registered as living in Hamburg in an area of 755.3&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (291.6&#160;sq&#160;mi). The <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">population density</a> was 2,464/km<sup>2</sup> (6,380/sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Hamburger_Melderegister_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamburger_Melderegister-32">[31]</a></sup> The metropolitan area of the Hamburg region (<a href="/wiki/Hamburg_Metropolitan_Region" title="Hamburg Metropolitan Region">Hamburg Metropolitan Region</a>) is home to 5,107,429 living on 196/km<sup>2</sup> (510/sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were 915,319 women and 945,440 men in Hamburg. For every 1,000 males, there were 1,033 females. In 2015, there were 19,768 births in Hamburg (of which 38.3% were to unmarried women); 6422 marriages and 3190 divorces, and 17,565 deaths. In the city, the population was spread out with 16.1% under the age of 18, and 18.3% were 65 years of age or older.<sup id="cite_ref-statistisches_jahrbuch_Hamburg_2016.2F2017_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statistisches_jahrbuch_Hamburg_2016.2F2017-34">[33]</a></sup> 356 People in Hamburg were over the age of 100.<sup id="cite_ref-Statistikamt_Nord_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Statistikamt_Nord-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the Statistical Office of the State of Hamburg, the number of people with a migrant background is at 34% (631,246).<sup id="cite_ref-statistik-nord.de_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statistik-nord.de-36">[35]</a></sup> Immigrants come from 180 different countries. 5891 people have acquired German cititzenship in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-statistik-nord.de_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statistik-nord.de-36">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, there were 1,021,666 households, of which 17.8% had children under the age of 18; 54.4% of all households were made up of singles. 25.6% of all households were single parent households. The average household size was 1.8.<sup id="cite_ref-Haushalte_nach_Stadtteilen_in_Hamburg_am_31.12.2016_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haushalte_nach_Stadtteilen_in_Hamburg_am_31.12.2016-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
